1. Run Summary

This section is the executive checksum for the run. It records the run ID, verifies the most important ordering constraint, and defines the report's audit colors. The key process guarantee is that literature search must occur before hypothesis generation, so the generated idea is grounded in retrieved papers rather than produced from the input PDF alone. Read GREEN/YELLOW/RED as claim-audit labels, not as paper-quality labels.

Run ID: b1d3d9bd-286d-4785-bd21-b0ca2ad7915e

Literature search happened before hypothesis generation: YES.

GREEN labels require verified references, bibliographic score >= 0.75, direct support, evidence snippets, provenance, and a written reason. The score column in the verification table is bibliographic identity confidence, not support strength. YELLOW means uncertain, partial, or a novel claim that still needs experiment; RED means not found, unsupported, contradicted, or clearly wrong.

Direct evidenceReference identity is verified, evidence explicitly addresses the claim, and the reason explains the support relation.
Indirect evidenceEvidence is relevant background or motivation, but it does not directly prove the full claim.
Background / absentEvidence is too weak, abstract-only, missing, or only useful as context.
Contradictory evidenceEvidence conflicts with the claim or contains explicit contradiction cues.

Four Error Categories

Unsupported ClaimThe conclusion is not supported by any available input material.
OverclaimThe material supports a weaker conclusion, but the generated claim states a stronger one.
Mis-citationThe cited material is topic-related but does not support the specific claim.
ContradictionThe generated claim conflicts with the cited material.

2. Input Paper Profiles

This section shows the structured profile extracted from each submitted PDF before any external retrieval or hypothesis generation. A profile contains the detected title, authors, abstract, and high-level source claims. These fields are produced by deterministic PDF parsing and profiling heuristics, with optional LLM assistance only when enabled. They are not treated as final truth: if the title, abstract, author list, or key claims are wrong, later search queries, relatedness filtering, literature-map items, and citation audits should be interpreted cautiously.

Clean profileTitle, authors, abstract, and key claims match the PDF; downstream retrieval is likely aligned.
Partial profileSome fields are missing or noisy; search may still work, but map and hypothesis quality should be reviewed manually.
Bad profileTitle/abstract/key claims are visibly wrong; later sections may be answering the wrong paper and should be treated as unreliable.

GENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPING

Authors:

Abstract: By employing neural networks (NN) to learn input-solution mappings and passing a new input through the learned mapping to obtain a solution instantly, recent studies have shown remarkable speed improvements over iterative algorithms for solving optimization problems. Meanwhile, they also highlight methodological challenges to be addressed. In particular, general non-convex problems often present multiple optimal solutions for identical inputs, signifying a complex, multi-valued input- solution mapping. Conventional learning techniques, primarily tailored to learn single-valued mappings, struggle to train NNs to accurately decipher multi-valued ones, leading to inferior solutions. We address this fundamental issue by developing a generative learning approach using a rectified flow (RectFlow) model built upon ordinary differential equations. In contrast to learning input-solution mapping, we learn the mapping from input to solution-distribution, exploiting the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model. Upon receiving a new input, we employ the trained RectFlow model to sample high-quality solutions from the input- dependent distribution it has learned. Our approach outperforms conceivable GAN and Diffusion models in terms of training stability and run-time complexity. We provide a detailed characterization of the optimality loss and runtime complexity associated with our generative approach. Simulation results for solving non-convex problems show that our method achieves significantly better solution optimality than recent NN schemes, with comparable feasibility and speedup performance.

Key claims:

  • Our approach outperforms conceivable GAN and Diffusion models in terms of training stability and run-time complexity.
  • Simulations show that our approach achieves significantly better solution optimality compared to recent NN schemes, while maintaining comparable speedup.
  • In contrast to learning input-solution mapping, we learn the mapping from input to solution-distribution, exploiting the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model.
  • Our approach is predicated on the availability of a training dataset sampled from a preferred input-dependent solution distribution, and leverages the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model to learn the distribution.

3. Paper Index Legend

This section defines the shorthand paper labels used throughout the report. Labels such as P1, P2, ... refer to retrieved or connected papers after deduplication and relatedness filtering; the internal JSON index remains zero-based, so P1 corresponds to JSON index 0. The relation field explains why a paper entered the candidate pool, for example input seed, cited by the input paper, citing the input paper, semantic expansion, or topical search. The relatedness score is the gate score used before map and hypothesis generation; it is not a citation-support score.

Distance 0 / input seedThe submitted paper itself; useful as context but not enough to prove a new idea.
Distance 1-2Cited-by or citing-neighborhood papers; these are stronger candidates for connected literature.
Semantic expansionTitle/abstract-near papers added when citation graph is sparse; useful but weaker than citation-chain evidence.
Low relatednessRejected or marginal papers should not support hypotheses, even if they are real papers.

4. Literature Map Summary

This section turns the connected paper pool into a literature map. A thread is a cluster of papers that share a method, problem setting, or conceptual role. A gap is a missing test, boundary condition, mechanism, or application regime suggested by the connected evidence. A tension records an unresolved contrast, such as two papers making claims that are hard to compare directly or using incompatible assumptions. A cross-domain connection is a possible transfer of an idea from one setting to another. These items are generated from paper titles, abstracts, retrieved snippets, and evidence IDs; they are the immediate inputs to hypothesis generation, not final claims by themselves.

Thread onlyThe literature clusters cleanly, but may not yet imply a new research question.
Gap + evidenceA plausible place to generate a hypothesis; check evidence IDs before trusting it.
TensionA stronger idea source when assumptions, benchmarks, or claims do not line up across papers.
Empty tension/connectionNot a failure by itself; it means the retrieved literature did not expose a clear contrast or transfer opportunity.

Map generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned a valid literature map with at least one thread and one gap.

Threads

  • T1 Generative modeling for multi-valued input-solution mappings: A research thread that uses generative models (RectFlow, diffusion, normalizing flows) to learn distributions over solutions conditioned on inputs, addressing the challenge of multi-valued mappings in non-convex optimization. Key papers: P1 (RectFlow), P6 (DiffOPF), P7 (Glow), P10 (NAF).
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 5 Evidence 7 Evidence 31 Evidence 32
  • T2 Augmented learning for disambiguating multi-valued mappings: A thread that augments the input space (e.g., with initial points) to convert a multi-valued mapping into a single-valued one, enabling standard supervised learning. Key papers: P3, P4, P5 (DeepOPF-AL).
    Evidence: Evidence 23 Evidence 20 Evidence 27
  • T3 Feasibility enforcement in neural optimization solvers: A thread focused on ensuring that neural network outputs satisfy problem constraints, using techniques like homeomorphic projection. Key papers: P11 (homeomorphic projection).
    Evidence:

Gaps

  • G1: No existing work combines generative modeling of solution distributions with explicit feasibility guarantees. P1 and P6 sample solutions but do not guarantee constraint satisfaction; P11 guarantees feasibility but uses a deterministic mapping, not a generative distribution. Why it matters: For safety-critical applications (e.g., power grids), both solution diversity and strict feasibility are required. A generative model that provably outputs feasible solutions would be highly impactful.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 31
  • G2: The sample complexity of generative solvers (RectFlow, diffusion) for non-convex problems is not characterized beyond the OPF domain. P1 provides theoretical optimality loss bounds but not sample complexity; P6 explores sample complexity only for OPF. Why it matters: Understanding sample complexity is crucial for practical deployment, as it determines the amount of training data required and the computational cost.
    Evidence: Evidence 11 Evidence 33
  • G3: No comparison between generative approaches (RectFlow, diffusion) and augmented learning (DeepOPF-AL) on the same benchmark problems. P1 compares to GANs and diffusion but not to augmented learning; P3, P4, P5 compare to standard DNNs but not to generative models. Why it matters: A head-to-head comparison would reveal which paradigm (generative vs. augmented) yields better optimality, feasibility, and speed for multi-valued problems.
    Evidence: Evidence 7 Evidence 20

Tensions

  • X1: Generative approaches (P1, P6) learn full solution distributions, while augmented learning (P3, P4, P5) collapses the multi-valued mapping to a single-valued one by augmenting inputs. The former preserves solution diversity, the latter sacrifices it for simplicity.
    Evidence: Evidence 5 Evidence 23
  • X2: P1 claims RectFlow outperforms diffusion models in training stability and runtime, but P6 uses diffusion for OPF and reports favorable results. The tension is whether diffusion models can be made stable and efficient enough for non-convex optimization, or if flow-based models are inherently superior.
    Evidence: Evidence 7 Evidence 32

Cross-Domain Connections

  • C1: The idea of learning input-dependent solution distributions from generative models (RectFlow, diffusion) in optimization can be transferred to inverse problems in scientific domains (e.g., astrophysics, medicine) where multiple parameter sets explain the same measurement. Rationale: P8 uses invertible neural networks for ambiguous inverse problems, learning posterior distributions. Generative optimization solvers (P1, P6) similarly learn conditional distributions over solutions. The methodology of conditional sampling via flows/diffusion can be directly applied to inverse problems to capture multi-modal posteriors.
    Evidence: Evidence 5 Evidence 31
  • C2: The augmented learning technique (augmenting input with initial points) from power systems (DeepOPF-AL) can be transferred to other engineering domains with multi-valued input-solution mappings, such as structural design optimization or chemical process optimization. Rationale: P3, P4, P5 explicitly state that augmented learning can be applied to various problems with multi-valued mappings. The method is domain-agnostic: augment input with a parameter that disambiguates the solution branch.
    Evidence: Evidence 20

5. Selected Hypothesis Portfolio

This section is the short answer: it presents the selected research-idea portfolio. By default the report keeps up to two hypotheses: a primary proposal and, when available, a distinct secondary or more incremental proposal. Each hypothesis is generated from the literature map and connected evidence, then scored for literature support, novelty, feasibility, cross-domain value, risk, obvious-extension penalty, and prior-work similarity. The primary hypothesis is used for novelty search and citation audit below. The secondary hypothesis is retained as a usable alternative, not as proof that it is equally novel or equally well supported.

Primary + high noveltyBest candidate for a new research direction, subject to novelty-search caveats.
Primary + high riskInteresting but fragile; prioritize feasibility and falsification before presenting it as a project.
Incremental secondaryUsable fallback or extension; expected to be less novel but often easier to test.
No evidence IDsIdea is mostly model inference; it should not be treated as source-supported.

Hypothesis generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned valid hypothesis candidates after binding and review.
Review: {'used': True, 'reason': 'LLM review returned replacement/refined hypotheses.', 'candidate_count': 2}

primary

H1 - RectFlow-based generative solvers can be extended to guarantee feasibility of sampled solutions by incorporating a homeomorphic projection layer, achieving both solution diversity and strict constraint satisfaction.

Total score: 0.49 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.7 Feasibility: 0.5 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.3

Why retained: Primary proposal selected for the main novelty search and citation audit. It is the strongest non-incremental candidate when available; otherwise it is the highest-scoring distinct candidate.

Motivation: P1 shows RectFlow learns solution distributions but does not guarantee feasibility; P11 provides a homeomorphic projection method that ensures feasibility for NN solutions. Combining these could yield a generative solver with provable feasibility, addressing G1.

Novelty position: No existing work combines generative distribution learning with explicit feasibility guarantees; this would be a novel integration.

How to test it: Train a RectFlow model on a non-convex OPF dataset, then apply homeomorphic projection to each sampled solution. Compare optimality, feasibility rate, and runtime against vanilla RectFlow and DeepOPF-AL.

Premise evidence: Evidence 5 Evidence 6 Evidence 7

Supporting papers: P1: GENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPING P11: Low Complexity Homeomorphic Projection to Ensure Neural-Network Solution Feasibility for Optimization over (Non-)Convex Set

Risks:

  • Projection may distort the learned distribution, reducing solution quality.
  • Computational overhead of projection may increase runtime.
incremental

H2 - The sample complexity of RectFlow-based generative solvers for non-convex problems scales polynomially with the dimension of the solution space, similar to diffusion models in OPF.

Total score: 0.33 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.6 Feasibility: 0.7 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.4

Why retained: Retained as a more conservative A+B, transfer, or implementation-adjacent direction that is distinct from the primary proposal. It may be useful follow-up work, but it should not be read as the most novel hypothesis.

Motivation: P1 provides optimality loss bounds but not sample complexity; P6 explores sample complexity for diffusion in OPF. Understanding sample complexity for RectFlow is crucial for practical deployment (G2).

Novelty position: First characterization of sample complexity for RectFlow in non-convex optimization, extending diffusion-based analysis.

How to test it: Train RectFlow models with varying training set sizes on a suite of non-convex problems (e.g., OPF, structural design). Measure optimality gap vs. training size and fit scaling laws.

Premise evidence: Evidence 11 Evidence 33

Supporting papers: P1: GENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPING P6: DiffOPF: Diffusion Solver for Optimal Power Flow

Risks:

  • Sample complexity may be problem-dependent and hard to generalize.
  • Theoretical bounds may be loose.

6. Hypothesis Quality Audit

This section audits the generated idea itself, not just the references attached to it. The hypothesis is decomposed into components such as idea core, motivation, novelty position, mechanism, feasibility, experiment, and risks. Each component receives a small relevance-ranked evidence bundle when directly relevant evidence exists; otherwise it is labeled as model-inferred or needing review. This avoids padding the hypothesis with weak citations while still showing which parts are grounded in evidence IDs.

Source-stated + GREENThe component restates source text with provenance; it is evidence, not novelty.
Evidence-inspired + YELLOWThe component is motivated by evidence but goes beyond what papers directly prove.
Model-inferred + YELLOWThe component is a generated bridge or mechanism; it needs human review and experiments.
Unsupported / REDThe component lacks evidence, contradicts evidence, or makes an unsupported strong claim.

Summary: Hypothesis quality audit separates what the literature directly says from what the model inferred as a new idea. Generated idea, novelty, and experiment components should usually be YELLOW unless directly restating an established finding.

Components: 16; by status: {'needs_human_review': 10, 'evidence_inspired': 6}; by color: {'yellow': 16}; precision evidence per component: None

literature map G2: context
In this paper, instead of learning input-solution mappings as in prior arts, we employ Rectified Flow (RectFlow) (Liu et al., 2022) as a generative approach to learn the mapping from input to a preferred solution distribution for solving non-convex problems. Our approach is predicated on the availability of a training dataset sampled from a preferred input-dependent solution distribution, and leverages the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model to learn the distribution. The results show that the probability that the generated solution incurs an optimality gap larger than any δ > 0 decreases exponentially to zero as we sample more from the learned input-dependent solution distribution.

literature map C1: context
Existing deep learning OPF solvers are single-valued and thus fail to capture the variability of system parameters unless fully represented in the feature space, which is prohibitive. To solve this problem, we introduce a diffusion-based OPF solver, termed DiffOPF, that treats OPF as a conditional sampling problem. The solver learns the joint distribution of loads and dispatch setpoints from operational history, and returns the marginal dispatch distributions conditioned on loads.

To solve this problem, we introduce a diffusion-based OPF solver, termed DiffOPF, that treats OPF as a conditional sampling problem. The solver learns the joint distribution of loads and dispatch setpoints from operational history, and returns the marginal dispatch distributions conditioned on loads. Unlike single-valued solvers, DiffOPF enables sampling statistically credible warm starts with favorable cost and constraint satisfaction trade-offs.

Existing feasibility-ensuring methods either are computationally expensive or lack performance guarantee. In this paper, we propose homeomorphic projection as a low-complexity scheme to guarantee NN solution feasibility for optimization over a general set homeomorphic to a unit ball, covering all compact convex sets and certain classes of nonconvex sets. The idea is to (i) learn a minimum distortion homeomorphic mapping between the constraint set and a unit ball using an invertible NN (INN), and then (ii) perform a simple bisection operation concerning the unit ball so that the INN-mapped final solution is feasible with respect to the constraint set with minor distortion-induced optimality loss.

7. Hypothesis Novelty Search

This section searches for close prior work after the selected hypothesis is generated. After the primary hypothesis is selected, the system creates novelty-search queries from the hypothesis text, method terms, target problem, and supporting-paper neighborhood, then searches external literature again. The result is a risk assessment: exact/close matches suggest the idea may already exist, near matches suggest the idea needs sharper differentiation, related works provide background, and missing results only mean no close prior work was found by available tools. This section never proves novelty conclusively.

High riskExact or close prior work found; the idea likely needs substantial differentiation.
Medium riskNear work shares method, target problem, or framing; novelty may survive only with a sharper mechanism.
Low riskOnly background-related work found; still not proof of novelty.
Unknown riskSearch failed or evidence is too sparse; do not claim novelty.

Novelty risk: MEDIUM

Conclusion: Similar prior work was found; the idea may need sharper differentiation.

Reason: 1 near match(es) share method, target problem, or abstract-level framing.

8. Cited Reference Integrity Audit

This section audits only the references actually cited by the selected hypothesis atomic claims. It answers a bibliographic question: does the cited reference appear to exist, and do title, authors, year, DOI, venue, or source URL point to the same paper? It deliberately does not judge whether the reference supports the scientific claim; that is handled in the next section. Field status values separate matched/present metadata from missing or uncertain metadata. Missing DOI/year usually means the field was unavailable or not comparable, not automatically that the reference is wrong.

Score is bibliographic identity confidence, not a metadata-completeness percentage. A searched bibliographic record or local input PDF can score highly even when DOI or year is missing, but non-seed references with both DOI and year missing are downgraded to YELLOW.

GREEN integrityReference identity is verified enough for audit use; claim support is still judged separately.
YELLOW integrityReference probably exists but metadata is incomplete or uncertain; support cannot be upgraded to strong confidence.
RED integrityReference is not found or metadata conflicts strongly; related claim-support rows should be RED.
Missing DOI/yearUsually source coverage limitation, not a contradiction, unless other metadata also conflicts.

This section audits whether each cited reference supports the specific atomic claim attached to it. Rows in this table are claim-reference pairs, so the same claim ID may appear more than once if multiple references are checked separately. This is stricter than bibliographic integrity: a real paper can be GREEN in the previous section but YELLOW or RED here if it only provides background, supports a weaker claim, discusses a different topic, or lacks usable evidence snippets.

Support type rule: direct means verified reference + explicit evidence for the specific claim; indirect means topic-relevant evidence that motivates but does not prove the claim; background means weak/abstract-only context; absent means no usable evidence or no valid reference.

Error category rule: no_specific_error_detected means this row was not classified as unsupported claim, overclaim, mis-citation, or contradiction. It does not mean the claim is proven; use Color, Support, Support Type, Evidence IDs, and Reason for the final interpretation.

GREEN + directVerified reference and explicit evidence directly support this exact claim.
YELLOW + indirect/backgroundEvidence motivates or contextualizes the claim but does not prove it.
RED + unsupported/absentNo usable evidence, invalid reference, contradiction, or clear unsupported claim.
no_specific_error_detectedNo four-category error was detected; this can still be YELLOW if support is indirect.

10. Evidence Cards

Evidence Cards expand evidence IDs into the human-readable form used throughout the report. Each card shows the source paper, URL or path, location, snippet text, and every claim or map item that reused the evidence. The same snippet can support multiple claims in different ways, so each use has its own support type, error category, and reason. Internal hash IDs are kept only in Appendix B for reproducibility; the numbered Evidence labels are intended for review.

One evidence, one useSimple case: one snippet is used for one claim or map item.
One evidence, multiple usesEach use has its own support type and reason; do not infer one global label for the snippet.
Abstract locationUseful but shallow; direct support from abstract alone should be interpreted cautiously.
PDF/full-text locationUsually stronger provenance because the snippet comes from a section or page-level source.

Evidence 11

Source paper: GENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPING

Source URL / path: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_044009.pdf

Location: pdf:method:p2; Used by: H2 premise, literature map G2

Used bySupport typeError categoryReason
H2 premisecontextno_specific_error_detected
literature map G2contextno_specific_error_detected
In this paper, instead of learning input-solution mappings as in prior arts, we employ Rectified Flow (RectFlow) (Liu et al., 2022) as a generative approach to learn the mapping from input to a preferred solution distribution for solving non-convex problems. Our approach is predicated on the availability of a training dataset sampled from a preferred input-dependent solution distribution, and leverages the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model to learn the distribution. The results show that the probability that the generated solution incurs an optimality gap larger than any δ > 0 decreases exponentially to zero as we sample more from the learned input-dependent solution distribution.

Evidence 31

Source paper: DiffOPF: Diffusion Solver for Optimal Power Flow

Source URL / path: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2510.14075.pdf

Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Used by: literature map T1, literature map G1, literature map C1

Used bySupport typeError categoryReason
literature map T1contextno_specific_error_detected
literature map G1contextno_specific_error_detected
literature map C1contextno_specific_error_detected
Existing deep learning OPF solvers are single-valued and thus fail to capture the variability of system parameters unless fully represented in the feature space, which is prohibitive. To solve this problem, we introduce a diffusion-based OPF solver, termed DiffOPF, that treats OPF as a conditional sampling problem. The solver learns the joint distribution of loads and dispatch setpoints from operational history, and returns the marginal dispatch distributions conditioned on loads.

Evidence 32

To solve this problem, we introduce a diffusion-based OPF solver, termed DiffOPF, that treats OPF as a conditional sampling problem. The solver learns the joint distribution of loads and dispatch setpoints from operational history, and returns the marginal dispatch distributions conditioned on loads. Unlike single-valued solvers, DiffOPF enables sampling statistically credible warm starts with favorable cost and constraint satisfaction trade-offs.

Existing feasibility-ensuring methods either are computationally expensive or lack performance guarantee. In this paper, we propose homeomorphic projection as a low-complexity scheme to guarantee NN solution feasibility for optimization over a general set homeomorphic to a unit ball, covering all compact convex sets and certain classes of nonconvex sets. The idea is to (i) learn a minimum distortion homeomorphic mapping between the constraint set and a unit ball using an invertible NN (INN), and then (ii) perform a simple bisection operation concerning the unit ball so that the INN-mapped final solution is feasible with respect to the constraint set with minor distortion-induced optimality loss.

11. Controlled Citation Audit Cases

This section is a controlled capability demonstration, separate from the selected hypothesis. The system constructs one direct-support case, one uncertain or inferred case, and one deliberately wrong or nonexistent-reference case. These probes show whether the audit machinery can produce GREEN, YELLOW, and RED under known conditions. They should not be interpreted as extra scientific claims made by the hypothesis generator.

GREEN controlled caseKnown direct support case; should have evidence and pass as GREEN.
YELLOW controlled caseGenerated or inferred claim; should not be forced to GREEN.
RED controlled caseFake reference, contradiction, or unsupported absolute claim; should never become GREEN.

14. Memory Influence Summary

This section explains the role of persistent SQLite memory in the current run. Memory can store prior papers, references, hypotheses, evidence records, rejected evidence, and verification outcomes. Retrieval does not automatically become evidence: previous memory may influence query planning, flag duplicate-risk, or warn about previously rejected material, but direct verification is reused only when the current claim, reference, evidence, and rule version match. If memory was retrieved but did not materially affect the run, the report says so explicitly.

Retrieved + materially alteredMemory changed query planning, duplicate-risk assessment, or direct verification reuse.
Retrieved + not usedMemory was available but treated as context only; this is acceptable and should be reported honestly.
Duplicate risk highA similar previous hypothesis exists; novelty should be interpreted cautiously.
Negative memoryPreviously rejected snippets or noisy candidates are warning signals, never scientific counter-evidence by themselves.

Memory influenced query planning/context through same-scope paper hits or memory-derived queries.

Negative Memory

These are previous rejected evidence snippets or failed candidates. They are warning-only memories: useful for avoiding repeated retrieval mistakes, identifying noisy sources, or explaining why a candidate was ignored, but never evidence that a scientific claim is false. Scientific support and contradiction must still come from current, inspectable evidence snippets.

16. Warnings and Limitations

This section collects automatic warnings and limitations detected during the run. Warnings are not automatic failures, but they mark places where a human reviewer should be cautious: shallow evidence, few retrieved papers, all-YELLOW claim support, high novelty risk, weak memory influence, API fallback, noisy reference extraction, or generated claims that remain mostly inferred.

No warningsThe automated checks found no obvious limitation, but human review is still required.
Evidence warningsSupport may be shallow, abstract-only, or missing full-text provenance.
Retrieval warningsFew connected papers, API fallback, or noisy search means the map may be incomplete.
Hypothesis warningsThe final idea may be mostly inferred, low-scoring, or duplicate-risk.
  • Evidence depth is limited: most evidence snippets come from abstracts rather than full text.
  • Some selected hypotheses are grounded only in input-paper evidence (H1); treat them as within-paper extrapolations unless external supporting papers are added.
  • Generated hypothesis support is mainly indirect, background, or absent.

Real API mode depends on public endpoints, available abstracts/full text, and external service coverage. Absence of retrieved close prior work is not proof of novelty.

10(E-63cb0b8cc762)full_text_pdfinput_pdfGENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPINGpdf:abstract:p10.95By employing neural networks (NN) to learn input-solution mappings and passing a new input through the learned mapping to obtain a solution instantly, recent studies have shown remarkable speed improvements over iterative algorithms for solving optimization problems. In particular, general non-convex problems often present multiple optimal solutions for identical inputs, signifying a complex, multi-valued inputsolution mapping. Conventional learning techniques, primarily tailored to learn single-valued mappings, struggle to train NNs to accurately decipher multi-valued ones, leading to inferior solutions.
11(E-5e11f01396b0)full_text_pdfinput_pdfGENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPINGpdf:method:p20.95In this paper, instead of learning input-solution mappings as in prior arts, we employ Rectified Flow (RectFlow) (Liu et al., 2022) as a generative approach to learn the mapping from input to a preferred solution distribution for solving non-convex problems. Our approach is predicated on the availability of a training dataset sampled from a preferred input-dependent solution distribution, and leverages the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model to learn the distribution. The results show that the probability that the generated solution incurs an optimality gap larger than any δ > 0 decreases exponentially to zero as we sample more from the learned input-dependent solution distribution.
12(E-172c26269760)full_text_pdfinput_pdfGENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPINGpdf:method:p40.95By learning such a mapping and passing new input through the learned mapping to obtain a quality solution instantly, neural networks (NN) have shown remarkable speed improvements in solving convex problems over iterative solvers. However, non-convex problems may exhibit a complexity that allows multiple optimal solutions for identical inputs, resulting in multi-valued input-solution associations. Consequently, the training dataset might include “mixed” data points, reflecting this “one-to-many” mapping.
13(E-40dd8050bf7a)full_text_pdfinput_pdfGENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPINGpdf:method:p40.95For strictly-convex problems, it has a unique optimal solution x∗ c for each input c, corresponding to a single-valued input-solution mapping F : c →x∗ c (Pan et al., 2019; Donti et al., 2021; Amos, 2022). By learning such a mapping and passing new input through the learned mapping to obtain a quality solution instantly, neural networks (NN) have shown remarkable speed improvements in solving convex problems over iterative solvers. However, non-convex problems may exhibit a complexity that allows multiple optimal solutions for identical inputs, resulting in multi-valued input-solution associations.

To solve this problem, we introduce a diffusion-based OPF solver, termed DiffOPF, that treats OPF as a conditional sampling problem. The solver learns the joint distribution of loads and dispatch setpoints from operational history, and returns the marginal dispatch distributions conditioned on loads.

Evidence Snippets with Provenance

CL1 - RED

Existing feasibility-ensuring methods either are computationally expensive or lack performance guarantee. In this paper, we propose homeomorphic projection as a low-complexity scheme to guarantee NN solution feasibility for optimization over a general set homeomorphic to a unit ball, covering all compact convex sets and certain classes of nonconvex sets. The idea is to (i) learn a minimum distortion homeomorphic mapping between the constraint set and a unit ball using an invertible NN (INN), and then (ii) perform a simple bisection operation concerning the unit ball so that the INN-mapped final solution is feasible with respect to the constraint set with minor distortion-induced optimality loss.
Evidence ID: 49 (E-8df1866f9269); Source: openreview; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Low Complexity Homeomorphic Projection to Ensure Neural-Network Solution Feasibility for Optimization over (Non-)Convex Set; URL: https://openreview.net/pdf/8c3f19cb2372a2274a916fba7a99884fdcd97b72.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
There has been growing interest in employing neural network (NN) to directly solve constrained optimization problems with low run-time complexity. Existing feasibility-ensuring methods either are computationally expensive or lack performance guarantee. In this paper, we propose homeomorphic projection as a low-complexity scheme to guarantee NN solution feasibility for optimization over a general set homeomorphic to a unit ball, covering all compact convex sets and certain classes of nonconvex sets.
Evidence ID: 50 (E-311a7156766b); Source: openreview; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Low Complexity Homeomorphic Projection to Ensure Neural-Network Solution Feasibility for Optimization over (Non-)Convex Set; URL: https://openreview.net/pdf/8c3f19cb2372a2274a916fba7a99884fdcd97b72.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
In this paper, we propose homeomorphic projection as a low-complexity scheme to guarantee NN solution feasibility for optimization over a general set homeomorphic to a unit ball, covering all compact convex sets and certain classes of nonconvex sets. The idea is to (i) learn a minimum distortion homeomorphic mapping between the constraint set and a unit ball using an invertible NN (INN), and then (ii) perform a simple bisection operation concerning the unit ball so that the INN-mapped final solution is feasible with respect to the constraint set with minor distortion-induced optimality loss. We prove the feasibility guarantee and bound the optimality loss under mild conditions.
Evidence ID: 51 (E-08ff7990f80b); Source: openreview; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Low Complexity Homeomorphic Projection to Ensure Neural-Network Solution Feasibility for Optimization over (Non-)Convex Set; URL: https://openreview.net/pdf/8c3f19cb2372a2274a916fba7a99884fdcd97b72.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
We prove the feasibility guarantee and bound the optimality loss under mild conditions. Simulation results, including those for non-convex AC-OPF problems in power grid operation, show that homeomorphic projection outperforms existing methods in solution feasibility and run-time complexity, while achieving similar optimality loss.

CL3 - YELLOW

Conventional learning techniques, primarily tailored to learn single-valued mappings, struggle to train NNs to accurately decipher multi-valued ones, leading to inferior solutions. We address this fundamental issue by developing a generative learning approach using a rectified flow (RectFlow) model built upon ordinary differential equations. In contrast to learning input-solution mapping, we learn the mapping from input to solution-distribution, exploiting the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model.
Evidence ID: 57 (E-21a9b4d51317); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: GENERATIVE LEARNING FOR SOLVING NON-CONVEX PROBLEM WITH MULTI-VALUED INPUT-SOLUTION MAPPING; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_044009.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
We address this fundamental issue by developing a generative learning approach using a rectified flow (RectFlow) model built upon ordinary differential equations. In contrast to learning input-solution mapping, we learn the mapping from input to solution-distribution, exploiting the universal approximation capability of the RectFlow model. Upon receiving a new input, we employ the trained RectFlow model to sample high-quality solutions from the inputdependent distribution it has learned.
